Et s’il vuet esventer som bras, et sainier autre fois, si covient, quant il est afoiblis par grant sainie, k’il se saine poc et poc, selonc la quantiteit de sa vertu continueement.	And if he sees that his arm has turned pale, and he feels well again another time, if it is possible by great bleeding, let it rest still and still, according to the quantity of its continuous virtue.
Et qui vuet esventer som bras aprés la premiere sainie, et il a cors fort, si se saine .vij. houres aprés la premiere sainie, ou aprés la nuevime houre.	And whoever has seen his arm swollen after the first bleeding, and has strong swelling, if he remains thus for thirteen hours after the first bleeding, or after the twelfth hour.
Et qui se vuet sainier d’autre part, si se doit sainier lou secont jor ou le tier.	And whoever has seen himself well on one side, if he must remain well for the second or third day.
Car il a mout de sanc dedens le cors qui est ja eschaufez, et fait venir fievre; si en covient traire grant quantiteit, et eslargir le pertus tant qu’il chiece em pameson; puis que tu rewardes toutes les conditions de sainie, et met ta main sus som poulz quant il saine, que li mors ne viegne en leu de pameson, et ceu avient maintes fois, quant li sainieres est negligens; et ne covient mie quant tu vues desliier lou bras, et faire issir sanc la seconde fois, et li pertus est clos, que tu presses forment sus; ne s’il est corbes, qu’il estende lou bras hastanment, car ce seroit malz; mais le laisse tant qu’il saine la seconde fois; ou tu i point a la pointe dou flaime, tant que li sans qui est congelez a pertus de la voinne isse fuer, et met sus I poc de seil remis en aigue, et presse sus legierement.	For there is blood in the body which is already evacuated, and causes fever; if it is to be drained in great quantity, and to dilate the belly until it cries out; then when you have restored all the blood, and put your hand over the pulse when it is sound, that the blood does not come out again in place of the vomit, and this often occurs when the patient is negligent; and it must not be done until you see the arm slip, and make the blood vomit for the second time, and the belly is closed, that you press firmly; if it is crooked, that you extend the arm as far as possible, because that would be bad; but let it go as soon as it comes back the second time; or you prick at the tip of the flame, until that which is frozen at the belly is evacuated from the belly, and put some oil on it and press lightly.
Et se li voine est enflee, si la laisse et ne l’estreindre mie, tant que li aposteme soit apaisiez.	And if the vein has escaped, if you leave it alone and do not restrain me, until the swelling subsides.
Et se necessitez est dou traire dou sanc la seconde fois, si le saine en celui leu meisme, ou en un autre voinne, ou en l’autre bras.	And if necessary is to drain blood the second time, if the same in this limb or in another vein, or in the other arm.
Li voinne emmi le bras est sainie en leu de la voine dou chief por ceu qu’elle est composte.	The vein in the arm is saved in place of the vein of the head because it is composed.
Et li maniere de la sainie si est que li malaides boute sa main en aigue chaude, tant que li foscile soit rouges; puis soit liiéz moienement, si que li voinne apere bien, puis le saine maintenant dou travers, ne mie dou large ne dou lonc; et soit li pertus larges, et soit sainiez bien pres de la jointe de la main, et se sans n’en puet issir, si remet la main en aigue chaude, et laisse corre lou sanc en l’aigue, tant que tu en aies assez; et se c’est en esteit, si ne rebouter mie la main en aigue chaude, car on l’i met plus en yver que en esteit; et li sainie de ceste voine est plus sauve de toutes autres voinnes, qu’il n’i a ne artere ne nerf.	And if the patient grasps his hand in hot water, while the blood vessel is red; then bind it moderately so that the wound opens well; then pull it out without haste, neither too wide nor too long; or make a large perforation and bind it closely to the joint of the hand, and if he cannot bear it, grasp the hand once more in hot water and leave the blood flow in the water until you have enough; and if it is in this state, do not withdraw the hand in hot water, for it is put in more danger thereby; the blood of this vein is more valuable than all other veins, for there is no artery or nerve.
Li sainie de la salvatele de la main destre si vaut a toutes les maladies dou foie, et c’est li maniere: estrain la main, et boute en aigue chaude, tant que li voinne soit enflee, si c’on la voie; et se la saine de travers, et se tu la copes dou tout en tout, si ne grieve rien; et n’aler mie trop em parfont, car li nerf sont desoz toz desnuez de char, et puis remet la main en aigue chaude, se li sans est congelez en la plaie, et n’en puet issir.	The blood of the salivary vein of the right hand is worth for all the diseases of the liver; and this is the method: strain the hand and grasp it in hot water, until the vein is emptied; if you see it, and if you pull it out, and if you press it throughout, if there is no pain; do not press too much, for the nerves are already exposed without covering. Then grasp the hand once more in hot water, if it is not frozen in the wound, and do not bear it.
Et quant tu avras trait dou sanc tant com mestier t’est, si met sus la voinne oile et seil por souder hastanment, et ensi fai de chescun rain.	And when you have treated the blood as much as necessary, if you put oil and lint on the wound, it will help to hold it together, and put on a bandage.</start_of_turn
Li sainie de li en la main senestre si vaut a la maladie de la mice, et fai tout ausi com je t’ai dit devers destre.	The blood in the left hand is worth to the disease of the mice, and do the same as I have said concerning the right side.</start_of_turn
Li sainie de la sophene si vaut a toutes les maladies que sont desoz le cors, si com maladies de la marris, et retenement de flors, et maladie des rains, et des cuixes et des jambes ancienes, et semblans maladies.	The blood of the spleen is worth for all the diseases which are caused by the heat of the body, such as diseases of the marrow, retention of flowers, diseases of the rains, and of the cramps and old legs, and similar diseases.
Et li qualitez de sa sainie si est que li malaides boute som piet en aigue chaude, et frote sus la voinne tant qu’elle pere, et lie desus la jointure dou piet; et li voinne si est a la cheville, et par desus lou piet en vont mout de rains; saine la donques ou plus large rain, ou a la cheville, que mues vaut.	And the quality of its blood is that when the patient is suffering from acute pain in the belly, and it is fanned over the abdomen until it bursts, and it is detached from the joint of the belly; and the abdomen is at the ankle, and from under the belly there comes out drops of rain; in the same way as a great or small rain, or at the ankle, that much is worth.
Et se tu la saines sus lou piet, si te warde des ners qui sont desoz, et la saine de traver ausi com en copant; et soit li flaimes anesil.	And if you touch it with your hand, if you feel numbness there, and if it is as if you were burning; be cautious.</start_of_turn>
Et se li sans n’en puet issir, si remet lou piet en l’aigue, et laisse bien vuidier la voine.	And if it does not recover, if you reset the limb in the water, and let the vein drain well.</start_of_turn>
Et se li sainieres faut la premiere fois, si rencomence la seconde au desus un poc, car li leus est salz; ne ne douter de nerf, si com nos avons dit, et ensi fai de la sophene de l’autre piet igalment.	And if the spleen must be extracted for the first time, if it starts again afterwards under an inch, because its blood is salty; do not doubt, as we have said, and do the same with the other side equally
Li leus de la voinne sciatique est a la cheville de fuer, et sa sainie vaut a la dolor de la hainche, quant c’est por sanc chaut.	The blood of the sciatic vein is at the ankle of force, and its blood is worth the pain of the groin, when it is for blood that is clotted.</start_of_turn>
Et li qualitez de sa sainie si est que li malaides entre ou bain, et estraigne desus la cheville IIII dois en tordant, car autrement n’apert li voinne; et s’elle apert, si le saine en toutes les manieres que tu pues, et en traversant; et vaut mues que tu la copes, car li leus est saus, et elle est mout reponue a mains homes.	And if the patient be between or in water, he must pull out the foot three times by twisting, for otherwise he will not open the vein; and if it opens, do so in all ways that you can, and through and through; and it is better that you stop it, for the wound is deep and the blood will not clot.
Et se tu ne la trueves, si saine aucun rain de li qui apert ou dos dou piet, et vers lou petit doi, et te warde des ners; et fai tant corre dou sanc que tu vues, et puis deslie lou lien, et met sus coton et l’estraint; ele est maintenant sanee.	If you do not find blood flowing from the opening or from behind the knee, and towards the heel, and you take care of the wound so that it does not open further, and you tie it up tightly and bind it; it is now healed.</start_of_turn>
des sainies de ventouse	of bloodletting
Ventouses sont fait de cornes, ou d’arain, ou de voirre; et ventosation si est faite selonc .ij. manieres: ou par ferir et atraire sanc, ou sens ferir.	Ventouses sont fait de cornes, ou d’arain, ou de voirre; et ventosation si est faite selon ces deux manieres: ou par ferir et atraire sang, ou sens ferir.
Et ceste ventosation qui est sens ferir si est selonc II manieres: ou elle est a tout feu, ou sens feu; et ventosation qui est faite par ferir et par estraction de sanc si est faite en XIIII leus dou cors, de coi li uns est tout en som le chief; et li autres est emmi le col; et II autres en II costeis dou col; et li ventouse desoz lou menton; et li ventouse des II espaules; et li ventouse sus les crupies; et les ventouses emmi les II bras; et les ventouses des II jambes; et les ventouses des II chevilles; et li ventosation ne trait point de sanc, fuer que des menues voinnes que sont esparses en la char, et por ceu n’en afoiblist om mie, si com on fait de sainie de voinne; et ne covient mie qu’elle soit aministree, ne a tout feu ne sens feu, en aucune maladie qui vient de repletion, tant que toz li cors soit vuidiez.	And this bleeding which is to be done has two methods: either with great heat or with cold; and the bleeding which is done by cutting and by extracting blood is done in twelve wounds of the body, of which one is at the top of the head; and one is at the collarbone; and two on the two ribs of the collarbone; and the ventouse on the chin; and two on the two shoulders; and one on the hips; and two on the two arms; and two on the two legs; and two on the two ankles; and the bleeding does not affect any major veins which are scattered throughout the body, for this is what we experience when we bleed from a vein; and it is only to be administered, neither with great heat nor with cold, in no disease which comes from repletion, as long as all the parts of the body are emptied.</start_of_turn>
Et se par necessiteit de maladies covient ventoser, ou por acostumances, si l’amenistrons chescune houre en l’encomencement dou mois, ou en la fin, et emmi et en chescun tens; car il i a de telz qui, quant il ont mestier de ventoser, si ont pesant chief; et si a telz qui truevent repletion et roujor en lor faice, et en lor chief, et au col, et tenebrour as eulz; et telz qui truevent une goute de sanc en lor bouche, et ont enflee la lengue, et raichent sanc; et si i a de telz qui songent, et voient par nut sanc et navrez et mors et semblans choses.	And if necessity of illness compels it, or by custom, if the physician sees a wound that has remained open for a whole month at the beginning or at the end, and throughout; for there are some who, when forced to rush, have a very heavy head; and some who find swelling and redness in their face, in their head, at the throat, and in their eyes; and some who have a drop of blood in their mouth, have lost the tongue, and spit blood; and some who think, and see nothing but blood and nausea and death and other things.
Et quant nos voions ces signes, et meismement quant c’est emmi lou mois, nos lou commandons a ventoser aprés .iij. houres dou jor.	And when we see these signs, and especially when it is wet and moist, we command them to fast for two hours after dinner.</start_of_turn>
Li sainie de la muze si aide a la grieteit dou chief … que vient as eulz, et ne covient mie que ce soit aprés l’evacuation de tout lou cors; et ceste ventosation si est en leu de la sainie de la cephalique, et ne covient mie que cil qui est de froide complexion en use, ou qui ait chatharre, car ce lor fait mout grant nuisement; et por ceu ne covient mie que li veillart en usent, ne qui ont ou chief froides maladies.	The blood of the physician helps with the grief of the head … that comes from the eyes, and it can only be done after the evacuation of all the fat; and this ventilation is useful instead of cephalic blood, and it can only be used by those who have a cold complexion or who have chills, because this causes them great discomfort; and for this reason it should only be used by those who have or have had cold fevers.
Et cil qui s’en saine sovent, si devient oblious, et por ce covient mettre la ventouse un poc plus aval par la doutance d’obliance.	And if the patient is conscious, if he becomes dizzy, it is necessary to place the suction tube one place further down the throat due to the doubt of dizziness.
Li ventosation emmi le col est en leu de la sainie dou chief et de la basilique, et por ceu vaut elle a l’estrecement dou pis, et a fendure de la chenal dou pormon, et a toust, et a repletion; et la covient poc bouter, car s’elle tient trop fermement, si enjanre obliance ou stomac et ou cuer.	The wind coming from the head is in place of blood and fat, and for this reason it is worth stretching out the thumb, and splitting the channel of the thumb, and everything together; and the consequence is that it must be pushed, because if it stays too tightly, it will cause discomfort or bloating of the belly or chest or stomach.
Li ventosations as II costelz dou col si vaut as dolors dou chief, et obtalmie et emigrainne, et a l’estranglement, et a la dolor as racines des dens, et est en leu de la sainie de la basilique; et covient que tu comandes au sainor qu’il espresse sa main au mettre, qu’il ne cope l’artere, et ne veigne decorremens de sanc.	The wind coming from the head causes pain in the chest, and swelling and itching, and constriction, and pain at the root of the teeth; and it is in place of the blood of the fat; and you must command the patient to express his hand to prevent him from cutting an artery or causing a loss of blood.
Li ventosations desoz lou menton si vaut a maladie de bouche, et a corruption de gencives, et semblans maladies qui sont en la bouche; et est en leu des sainies des .ij. levres.	The winds blow cold from the coast and cause disease of the mouth, and corruption of the gums, and similar diseases that are in the mouth; and it is in the place of the health of the two upper teeth.</start_of_turn>
Li ventosations des espaules vaut a maladie que vient de chalor et de remplissement.	The winds blow cold from the coast and cause disease that comes from heat and swelling.</start_of_turn
Li ventosations des coutes si vaut autant com des III voinnes dou bras, car elle atrait sanc de ces grosses voinnes; et comande au sainor qu’il ne fiere mie trop em parfont, car li leus est nus de char, et sait desoz ners et arteres.	The wind from the armpits is worth as much as three veins of the arm, because it draws blood from these large veins; and command the patient not to tighten them too much, because the flesh is soft, and knows bones and arteries
Li ventouse qui est sus la croupe si vaut as emoroydes dou fondement, et as maladies desoz, et covient que li ventouse soit grans, et soit d’arain, qu’elle a mestier de bien traire; et par aventure, s’elle estoit subtilz, si briseroit; et i fai grans plaies.	The wound on the buttocks is worth as much as sores of the foundation, and has diseases of these, and it is advised that the wound be large and of hair, that it has much to do; and by chance, if it should be superficial, it would break; and this would make great wounds.</start_of_turn
Les ventouses des jambes si sainent apertement, car elles atraient sanc de tout le cors, et valent as dolors ancienes des rains, et de la marris, et de la vessie, et ramoinnent les flors; et sont en leu des .ij. sophenes, et des .ij. chevilles, et toutevoie elles afoiblissent lou cors aprés lonc us, et font venir paumison em plusors dispositions.	The veins of the legs swell widely open, for they draw blood from all parts of the body, and cause ancient pains of the rains, and of the kidneys, and of the bladder, and restore the flowers; and are in the place of the groin, and of the knee, and all of them expand the body afterwards, and cause vomiting in various positions.
Li maniere de mettre la ventouse si est que tu la mettes premier vuide, et succe tempreement, et la met hastanment, et remue igalment, et ne cesser de ceu faire, tant que tu voies lou leu rouge et enfleit, et apere li roujor dou sanc; puis fier, et remet les ventouses, et pren warde en la disposition des cors, car il i a telz qui ont tanre char, et clers porez: si covient ferir une fois soulement, et covient faire larges plaies, et un poc parfondes; et suce legierement.	The way to apply the letup is that you apply it at once without delay, and do so steadily, and hold it firmly in place, and move it equally, and do not stop doing this until you see the limb turn red and swell, and expel the redness of blood; then withdraw, and reapply the letup, and take care of the position of the body, for there are many who have a tender scrotum, and clear pores: if you do but cut once only, and you make large wounds, and a large depth; and do so gently.</start_of_turn>
Et se li sans est gros, si couvient ferir .ij. fois: la premiere fois por subtiliier la voie dou sanc gros.	And if it is not thick, if you can cut twice: the first time to underlay the large blood vessel.</start_of_turn>
Et se li sans est tres espés, si covient ferir la tierce fois; et quant nos volons traire dou sanc poc et poc, si nos soffist soulement une fois.	And if it is not very painful, if you must inflict the wound for the third time; and when our wings are weary of carrying blood and blood, if our breath is stifled once.
Et se nos volons mout de sanc, si rencomensons sovent; et se li sans est espés, si fier em parfont, et li termines si est que li keurs soit perciez soudement, et couvient a celui qui a gros cuir et dur, et qui ont estrois porelz, que tu oignes les leus des ventouses d’oile aperitif et dissolutif: se c’est en esteit, d’oile violet, ou d’oile d’amandres douces, ou d’oile de semence de cucurbite; et se c’est en yveir, d’oile de cresson, ou de lis, ou de camomille, ou autre semblant.	And if our veins bleed excessively, if we repeat the process often; and if it is not expected, if it is fierce and pressing, and it lasts as long as it seems, it befits one who has thick skin and hard, and who has large pores, that you order the loosening of the winds of oil aperitif and dissolvent: if it is in summer, oil violet, or sweet almond oil, or oil of pumpkin seed; and if it is in winter, oil of rosemary, or sage, or chamomile, or any similar.
Et se li superfluitez est grosse et froide, si pren oile amaratin, ou de balsamite, ou oile d’anet, et les semblans.	And if the swelling is great and cold, if take oil of marigold, or balsam, or oil of ivy, and the like.</start_of_turn>
Et se cil cui on saine a tendre char et larges porelz, se n’i covient mettre point d’oile, mais le covient laver aprés la ventosation d’aigue rose, ou d’aigue froide, ou d’aigue de morelle, ou d’aigue de cucurbite, ou d’aigue de porcelainne, ou autre semblant.	And if one has stubborn pain and large pores, one must not put oil on, but it is necessary to wash after the evacuation of rose water, or cold water, or black bile, or pumpkin seed water, or porcelain water, or something similar.
Et cil qui a mout de moistor, si se leve d’aisil, ou d’aigue de mirte, et de sumac, et de semblans choses.	And he who has moisture in his mouth, if he rises easily, or from a surge of mint, and thyme, and similar things.
Et cil qui ont grosses superfluitez se levent de vin viel, ou d’aigue de seuc, ou de decoction d’anet ou de camomile, ou d’autres semblans; et ne se covient mie sainier de ventouse devant le bain ne em bain, mais aprés lou bain une houre ou II; et ne dorme mie aprés la ventosacion, et covient k’il se warse, quant il se doit ventoser et quant il est ventosez.	And he who has large superficial wounds shall rise from wine old, or from sea water, or from decoction of rue or chamomile, or from other things like these; and he must not keep his wound covered with a bandage before the bath, but after the bath for an hour or two; and he must not sleep after the ventosation, and he must know that he must rise when he should rise and when he is risen.
Et warde se cil qui se saine est coleriques, et se li sans est trop agus et enflamez; si covient qu’il preigne choses froides, si com pomes grenates et endives et laitues a l’aisil, et semblans choses; et menjuce pucins, et char de chastron, et viandes moistes.	And keep him warm he who is pale and cold, and if he is too cold and inflamed; if he must take cold things, as with grapes and radishes and lettuce at ease, and other things like these; and eat barley and broth, and moist meat.
Et cil qui a froide complexion, si preigne syrop de miel, et vin odorant moien, qui est entre viel et noveil, et menjuce poc, et menjuce pucins et petis oiselz et pijons; et lou jor qu’il se ventousera, si doit plus boivre que mengier; et covient doner a aucuns homes aucune fois triaicle ou muscate devant la ventosacion, ou aprés, por conforter les membres principalz; et soit subtiliiez li sans, mais il ne covient mie que cil qui sont chaut le boivent.	And he who has a pale complexion, if he takes honey syrup and scented wine medium, which is between old and new, and eat broth and eat barley and eat small birds and herbs; and when he should vomit, he must drink more than he eats; and it is not to give to any man at any time vinegar or mustards before or after the ventosation, but only he who is hot drinks it.
Les ventouses que sont mises sens ferir sont celles que sont mises sus lou foie, et sus la mice, et podagre, et sus lou ventre et la boude et les rains et la hainche, car cil membre ne soffrent mie ferir sus oulz, et ne le fait on fuer que por atraire sanc de membre em membre, si com nos mettons sus les mameles quant li neis sainne; por oster la viscositeit qui est au membre, si com nos metons ventouses sus le ventre et sus la boude por amenuisier le membre; et oste la dolor, quant il i a opilation ou piere, si oste par sa vertu atractive par aventure l’opilation, ou desaert la piere de son leu; et ensi fait ele quant on le met sus lou foie et sus la mice quant il i a ventositeit retenue, et ces ventouses sont aministrees vuides por sucier tant soulement; et aucune fois sont aministrees ensamble feu, et aucunes fois plainnes d’aigue tede, si com em maladies dou pleuresin, et en tel maniere c’on emplisse la ventouse d’aigue chaude soule, ou d’aigue ou il ait cuit herbes covenaubles a ceu.	The wounds which are made to bleed are those which are made upon the liver, and upon the spleen, and podagre, and upon the belly and the buttock and the groin and the heel, because these members do not suffer from any wound upon the outside, and they do it only to draw blood from a member to another, as we do when we put them upon the nipples when they do not bleed; to reduce the viscosity which is in the member, as we do when we put on compresses upon the belly and the buttock to strengthen the member; and to stop the pain, when there is an operation or a fracture, because it stops by its attractive power the operation, or releases the fracture from its place; and thus it makes elevation when we put it upon the liver and the spleen when there is retained viscosity. These compresses are administered empty without any liquid, and never administered together with liquid, except in cases of pleurisy, and in such a manner we fill the compress with warm water, or with water or there are herbs that are concomitant with this.
Li forme de la ventouse aministré a feu si est ci:	The form of the compress administered with fire is thus:
Et ait II dois de large et demi palme de parfont, et ait ou costeit entor lou mileu I petit pertus, ausi com por bouter une aguille, et soit d’arain, souef devant si qu’ele ne blece lou malaide au mettre sus lou membre; et ait em mileu une verge de travers d’arain ou de fer, por mettre une chandoile emprise, et en soient et grans et petites selonc les maladies et l’eaige de ces qui les prenent; car les ventouses des enfans sont autres que des vielz, et des maigres que des gras.	And he had two large and half palms of barley, or cost him as much as a whole mile for a little hole, as though to push out an arrow; and it may be of rye, chaff before if it does not please the patient to put it on the limb; and he put in the middle a twig of rye or iron, to put on a bandage, and there were some great and some small according to the ailment and the degree of those who took them; for the blisters of the intestines are other than mere skin, and the leanness other than fat.
Et doit on ensi mettre ceste ventouse sus lou membre: empren la chandoile de cire ou I linement, et le met emmi la ventouse au traver de la verge qui est emmi la ventouse, si que li clartez soit au fons de la ventouse qu’elle ne brule le malaide; et puis la met sus lou membre, et tes dois soit sus lou pertus que nos avons dit, si que quant on vuet, si en va li vapor, et oste on la ventouse; et emprent on le linement si com devant, et le rencomence sovent, se tu as mestier.	And one must put this sponge on the limb: grasp the bandage firmly or the linen, and put it inside the sponge through the twig that is inside the sponge, so that it does not burn the patient; and then put it on the limb, and two must be put either on the hole that we have said, or on the abscess if you see that it is going to burst; and grasp the sponge when it is seen, and withdraw the sponge. And grasp the linen as before, and do it often, if you are able.
En la ventouse donques qui est aministree em maladie de pleuresin, a tout aigue, n’a point de verge ne de pertus, et n’est amenistree fuer que quant ele est plaine d’aigue, et est mise sus lou membre tant soulement; et c’est li forme:	In the suction which is administered in the disease of pleuresin, at all sharpness, there is no vessel nor hole, and it is only administered when it is clear of sharpness, and is placed on the limb only slightly; and this is its form:
Sansues ne sont amenistrees fuer que en membres ou on ne puet mettre ventouses por lour petitece, si com levres et gencives, et semblans leus; et en leus desnuez de char, si com doi ou neis, et autres semblans; et li qualitez de l’user si est que quant tu trueves sansues que sont en aigues douces nettes sens porreture, si les pren; et les met une nut en aigue douce, tant qu’elles soient famillouses, et n’aient rien en ventres; et puis soit sainiez de voinne et de ventouse, puis frote le membre tant k’il soit rouges, et met sus les sansues; et quant elles seront plainnes, et cherront, se c’est leu ou on puisse mettre ventouse, se li met, car ce vaut mout aprés.	Suctions are not administered except in limbs where it is impossible to place a suction due to their small size, such as bones and teeth, and similar things; and in those stripped of hair, such as the groin or navel, and others; and it is necessary that when the practitioner finds sutures that are in soft and delicate tissue without any visible sign of puncture, if they take hold; they should apply a drop of sweet liquid until they are familiar, and have nothing in their stomachs; and then either drain the wound and the suction, or rub the limb until it is red, and then apply the sutures; and when they are full, and swollen, if it is possible to put on a suction, it should be put on, for this is worth more.
Se ceu non, si leve le leu d’aisil, et puis d’aigue, mout bien, et puis l’espreint; et se li leus saine trop aprés lou cors des sansues, si moille I drapel de lin en aigue froide, et met sus, tant que li sans soit estenchiés; et se li sans est multipliez, si met sus vitriole broié, ou gales, et semblans choses, tant qu’il soit estenchiez; ou tu met sor le leu moitiez de feves pelees, et les laisse tant qu’elles s’aerdent au leu, si estanchera maintenant; et se tu en as de plus mestier, si pren autres sansues.	If this does not suffice, if you lift the limb easily and then with force, moisten well, and then stretch it; and if the limb remains too long afterwards like the hair of the beard, if you pour water over ice, put on, until the hair is stretched out; and if the hair is numerous, put on broken glass or splints, and similar things, until it is stretched out; or you extract half of the hairs, and leave them until they burn out at the root, if you now stop. If you have more of a master, take other hairs.
Et se les sansues ne se vuellent prendre, si frote le leu de sanc fres, ou tu i fiche une aguille tant que li sans en isse, et puis les met sus, et tantost com elles sentent lou sanc, si s’aerdent; et quant tu vues qu’elles chieent, si poudre sus un poc d’aloen et de seil broiet, ou de cendre, si cherront maintenant.	If the hairs do not take hold, if you rub the limb with fresh blood, or you pierce it with a needle until the hair comes out, and then put on the dressing, and do so while they feel the blood, if it burns out; and when you see that they are bleeding, if you sprinkle over some aloes and bruised hempseed, or charcoal, they will stop now.
Ci comence li livre des brisures	This marks the beginning of the book of broken bones.
Cist chapistres est maintes fois necessaires en l’art de medicine, et est restorement de brisure et de desaluement.	This chapter is often necessary in the art of medicine, and is the restoration of fracture and displacement.
Saichiez, chier fil, que li fol mire se gietent de cest chapistre, qu’il ne virent onques nul des anciens livres, ne lettre n’en lurent; et por ceste cause est estraingie li science en nostre terre, car je n’en trovai onques nul boin; et n’en sai nient, fuer que par la science des anciens livres, ou j’ai tant estudiiet que je en ai traite la science, et par experiment, et par l’acostumance de ma vie; et je vos ai jai descrit en cest chapistre ceu qui est contenut, et l’ai abregié en la fin, et ai mostreit clerement les formes des estrumens que sont amenistrez, si com j’ai fait en ces II chapistres ci devant.	Hear now, my dear son, that in this chapter the learned men have made mention of things that no former book has ever contained, neither letter nor writing. And for this reason it is strange in our land, for I have never found anything like it; and I know of nothing save by the learned books of the ancients, or because I have studied them so thoroughly that I have mastered the science, and by experiment, and by the custom of my life. And I have described in this chapter what is contained in it, and I have abbreviated at the end, and I have made it clear the forms of the instruments that are mentioned, as I have done in the two chapters preceding.
Il covient donques que nos disons ou prologue de cest livre les sommes de la parole et les chapistres ou li saiges met son entendement, et demore sus la verteit, si que cil qui sont esproveit saichent et aprenent cest art noble, et l’aprenent a autrui.	It is necessary, therefore, that our words or prologue of this book should be an image of the truth and the chapters or lessons should place their understanding, and remain on the truth, so that those who are proved right learn and master this noble art, and learn it from others.
Je dis que quant brisure ou dislocation ou torsions ou quasseure vient a aucun, si covient que tu te hastes de sainier lou malaide d’une part et d’autre, s’aucune chose ne lou te tot, si com foiblece de cors, ou enfes, ou viellars; ou se li tens n’est tres chaus, ou trop frois; et menjuce cholz frois, c’est a savoir froides joutes, et char d’oiseil et de chevril, et ne boive point de vin, ne menjuce nules grasses chars, et ne s’emplisse mie trop de viandes qui remplissent les voinnes de sanc, tant que tu n’aies doute d’aposteme chaut, ne d’espandement de matiere au membre; si reviegne li malaides a som premerain governement k’il a useit.	I say that when there is a break or dislocation or twist or swelling that comes to any limb, if you must hasten the healing of the patient from one side and from the other, without doing anything else, if there is weakness in the bones, or in the flesh, or in the joints; or if the tension is not very strong, or too cold; and if you mix cold broth, that is to say cold baths, and oil and vinegar, and do not drink wine, do not eat fatty foods, and do not eat too much meat which fills the veins with blood, until you are certain that the patient has recovered from the pain, and from the swelling of the limb.
Et quant li os brisiés comence a restourer, si covient que on norrisse le malaide de viandes que font gros norrissement, ou il ait viscositeit, si com froment cuit en aigue, et ris, et testes, et piez, et pance de vaiche, et oefz, et poissons fres, et gros vin, et semblans chose.	And when the fractured bone begins to heal, if you must restore the patient, do not nourish the patient with meats that cause great swelling, or that have viscosity, such as wheat germ in water, and pork, and testicles, and spleen, and veins, and fish, and large wine, and similar things.</start_of_turn>
Car a teil norrissement se reprent mues et plus tost li brisure.	For in part nourishment returns muscle and more quickly the fracture breaks.
Et saichiez que li os brisiez des vielz et des decrepites ne se pueent mie bien reprendre ne souder selonc lor nature por la sechor des membres, ne cil ausi qui sont trop tendre, si com os de petis enfans; mais n’a cure, ramoinne sus l’os brisiet I gros tenrons qui s’aert et joint le membre ensamble, tant qu’il enforce si com il fut devant; et ne li tot point de son oevre, et por ceu le covient norrir de grosses viandes, si com nos avons dit.	And know that bones broken in joints and decrepit ones do not mend well without support of their nature by the drying of the limbs; for those that are too tough, like bones of small children; but there is no remedy, remain above the fractured bone the great tensions which have arisen and join the limb together, as long as it forces as it did before; and do not put any weight on it, for this is what we have said.
Et saiches que les briseures sont diverses em maintes manieres.	And know that fractures are of various kinds in many ways.</start_of_turn
Car li brisure dou chief est autre que li brisure dou pis, ou de la jambe, et je vos dirai en cest chapistre toutes manieres de brisure.	For the fire cautery, instruct the patient to let the hairs grow long and even; and if they prick at their base, hold the eyes steady so they do not move until they grow.
Car il i a brisure que vait a arvol, et autre en aguisant, si com li taille d’une penne por escrire.	For there are fractures that go to the marrow, and others by bending, as with the size of a pen to write.
Et si a brisure avec plaie, et brisure a tout une petite fendure.	And if there is a fracture with a wound, and a fracture with a whole small fissure.
Et a chescune maniere ait son engien, et son estrument propre en la restauration, selonc ceu que li remembrance vient en son leu; et est conue li brisure de l’os par ceu que li leus est tors, et apert bien par defuer, et sone au presseir de la main; et quant il n’i a tortuositeit ne son au tochier, et li malaides ne sent mie grant dolor, se n’i a point de brisure; mais il puet bien avoir tortuositeit, ou fendure, ou petite brisure.	And indeed, each method has its own instrument and its own restoration, according to what is remembered from its origin; and it is known that the fracture of a bone is recognized by its swelling, and it is opened well by pushing, and by the pressure of the hand; and when there is no swelling or pain, and the patient does not feel great pain, there is no fracture; but there may well be swelling or fissure or small fracture.
Si ne covient mie que tu lou mueves a la main, mais met sus medicines que vienent ci aprés, et puis l’estraint maintenant subtilment.	If it is not possible to move the limb by hand, but medicines come after, then hold it firmly now.
Et saiches que li brisure que vait en arvol, et sont les II parties desjointes, il les covient maintenant mettre a point igalment, ainsois que apostemes chaus i viegne; et se apostemes chaulz i vient, si lou laisse aucun jor, tant que li apostemes soit apaisiez; puis le met igal au mues que tu pues legierement et saigement, et saiches que li restauration de li est plus legiere que li restaurations de celi ou il ait pieces; puis l’estraint, si com il covient ci aprés.	And when the bladder breaks that goes into the belly, and they are the two parts of the joint, it is now necessary to put them back in place equally, as after an abscess has burst; and if an abscess bursts again, if you leave no afterbirth, until the abscess is appeased; then put them back equally to the muscle as you would do legitimately and cautiously, and the afterbirth of the latter is less burdensome than the restoration of the former or if there are parts missing; then strain, as it is necessary afterwards.
Et se en l’os a piéces brisies, si l’estent de II pars, se c’est mains ou piet, ou a ta main se c’est uns petis membres, ou a II cordes, ou a une corde et a ta main; et met le membre selonc sa figure natureil, si que quant les parties dou membre brisiet sont desevrees, si ramoinne par toz engiens les additions en lor leus au mues que tu pues, et garde que par ton ovraige ne viegne au malaide lesion ne doulor; et ajoste bien les II chiés des os selonc les millors formes.	And if there are broken pieces in the bone, if they extend from two parts, if it is the hand or foot, or at your hand if it is a small limb, or two strings, or a string and your hand; and put the limb back according to its natural figure, so that when the parts of the limb break they are loosened, if you rejoin everything in its place to the muscle as you would do, and keep that in your work does not cause the patient any injury or pain; and adjust well the two ends of the bone according to the best forms.
Et le sent bien a la main s’il te semble k’il ne soit mie bien: si la dresce bellement, et ne l’estraindre mie trop, ne presser si com font cil qui i font venir par lor ovraige aposteme chaut et contraction au membre, si com nos avons veut maintes fois.	If he feels well in the hand, though he seems unwell: if the swelling is beautiful and does not tighten too much, do not press as they do who make it come by their work at the height of swelling and contraction of the limb.
Et quant li membres est a point, si chastie bien lou malaide qu’il ne lou mueve, ne en veillant ne en dormant, ne en tornant, ne en autre maniere; et warde bien que li membres soit si asseurez qu’il ne sente nule dolor.	And when the limb is fixed in place, if the patient is very attentive, let him not move it, neither while awake nor asleep, neither by turning nor in any other way; and keep the limb firmly secured so that he feels no pain.
Car quant il sent nulle dolor, si n’est mie a point, et le covient mettre en teil disposition qu’il ne sent nule dolor; et te garde k’il soit mis droitement, que tortuositez n’i veigne quant il iert restorez; et li qualitez dou membre brisiet est selonc ceu que je te dis.	For when he feels no pain, if it is not at its proper place, and you must put it in a position where he feels no pain; and keep it straight, so that it does not twist when you restore it; and the quality of the limb depends on what I have told you.</start_of_turn>
Saiches que li membre brisiet sont divers em petitece et en grandor et en forme.	The limb is braced in various ways in small and large sizes and shapes.
Car il i a petis membres, si com brais et dois et semblans: si covient envoloupeir drapelz de lin molz et subtilz; et cil qui sont gros, si com cuixe et jambe: si covient envoloupeir d’un liien large et dur, car li larges liiens embraice tout le grant membre, et l’estraint de toutes pars igalment, si qu’il n’i a nule taiche; puis lie entor estoupes legieres, III tours ou IIII selonc lou membre, et estraint ta main un poc a tout le liien, puis vai au desus de la brisure et l’estraint moins, que la brisure puis esloigne un poc la brisure; et mollifie lou liien poc et poc, tant que tu preignes grant partie dou leu sain, puis repren I autre liien, et torne entor derechief sus lou leu brisiet; puis revai a la partie desoz de la brisure, et l’estraint moienement, ne mie trop fort ne trop molement, si com nos avons dit en la premiere lieure desus; puis met entre les liiens estoupes de lin ou drapelz, tant qu’il soit toz igalz, puis lie tout par desus I bendeil, et puis met tes esteles bien faites sus, s’il n’i a aposteme ou enfleure.	For there are small limbs like arms and thighs and similar: if you can wrap them with soft and thin cloths; and those that are large like hips and legs: if you can wrap them with a broad and strong cord, because the wide cord embraces the whole large limb and restricts it equally, so that there is no slack; then put on tight ligatures, three turns or four according to the limb, and strain your hand once over the whole limb, then go down to the break and strain less, that the break then slips; and soften the cord a little over and under, as long as you have taken a large part of the limb back, then take up another cord and strain again in place of the break; then put back to the underside of the break, and strain moderately, not too strong nor too weak, as we said at the beginning of this chapter; then put between the ligatures some strips of linen or cloths, so that they are even all around, and then tie everything securely with your hands, and then put your pins in place, if there is no incision or swelling.</start_of_turn
Et s’il i a enfleure ou aposteme, si met sus chose apaisant aposteme chaut, et qui oste l’enfleure, et le laisse ensi aucuns jors; puis estrain sus tes esteles, et soient de moitiet d’une large cainne rese saigement, ou de fust dont on fait les clives, ou de rains de palme, et soient selonc ceste figure:	If there is swelling or abscess, put upon something soothing the abscess hot, and which closes the swelling, and leave it there for several days; then strain upon your toes, and let them be half of a broad cane resting steadily, or of wood from which you make nails, or of barley straw, and let them be like this figure.
mais il covient que li estele que om met sus la brisure soit un poc plus grosse et plus large que les autres; et soit ausi longe com il covient au membre de petitece ou de grandor; puis estraint sus les esteles ausi com tu as fait a la premiere lieure, puis lie desus un fil avenanment, si com en la premiere lieure sus la briseure; plus estroit est ensus, adés moins; et covient que li fil soient moien, ne trop gros ne trop menus, et soient de lin mol; car se li fil sont gros, si com j’ai veut des oevres des folz qui font lor lieures des cordes de linement tort, et c’est grant error, car li lieure ne puet estre igal s’il est trop subtilz, et ne covient mie qu’il ait entre chescune estele moins dou doi d’espaice.	But it is necessary that the staff which you put upon the fracture be larger and broader than the others; and be as long as it is necessary for the limb to be small or large; then strain upon the toes as you did at the first hour, and then tie a thread steadily, as you did at the first hour upon the fracture; the tighter it is, the less; and it must be that the thread be neither too thick nor too thin, and be of linen. For if the thread is thick, as if I were to want to make the fools who make their knots of linen string, that is a great mistake, because the knot will not be equal if it is too tight, and it will not hold properly.
Et se li malaides est navrez par les chiés des esteles aprés l’estrignement en leus sains, si met desoz elles estoupes de lin, ou lainne charpie, si qu’il ne se blece.	If the patient is nauseated by the nails after stripping in the flesh, if you put down nails of linen or coarse cloth, it will not stain.
Quant li plaie est avec la briseure, et li keurs est rompus, je en dirai la cure ci aprés.	When the wound is with the splinter, and the nerve is fractured, I will speak of the cure later on.
Et saiches qu’il ne covient mie estraindre d’esteles toz les membres brisiez le premier jor.	The wounds that it is not possible to restrain with the hands must be grasped from the toes of the limbs which break first.
Car quant li membres est grans, se n’i covient mie mettre esteles fuer que aprés V jors ou VII, ou plus selonc ta seurteit d’aposteme chaut sorvenant; et vez ci la maniere des emplaustres anciiens por restorer brisure, dislocation, et torsion: et est que tu pren de la farine que se tient as parois dou molin, et la confis avec abun d’uef, et met ceste confection, ne trop clere ne trop espesse, et en use, et c’est li commune maniere de l’emplaustre restorant la brisure a plusors dispositions, et meismement as femmes et as petis enfans, car il n’est mie ne trop chas ne trop frois.	For when the limb is great, it is not possible to put these stakes in place except after five days or seven, or more according to your suspicion of the afterbirth coming out; and when this is done, the manner of ancient plasters to restore a fracture, dislocation, and torsion: take some flour that remains at the walls of a mill, and mix it with eggs, and make this confection, not too thick nor too thin, and use it. This is the common manner of plastering that restores a fracture in various positions, and even among women and young children, for it is neither too harsh nor too cold.
Autre maniere d’emplastre restorans la brisure et la dislocation et la torsion: pren З abareis et ladani et enule et asefetide et gomme arabique et mugat, ana .x. З.; mirre et aloes, ana .v. З.; alechil .viiij. З.; et en fai poudre, et les melle avec aubun d’uef; se li complexion de l’ome est chaude, si use de cest emplaustre, car il est de bone composition, et restoure les os brisiés hastanment, et vaut a plusors homes.	Another method of dressing that restores the fracture and dislocation and torsion: take three barley grains and linseed and almond and saffron and gum arabic and mugwort, all in the amount of three; mirra and aloe, in the amount of three; beeswax, eight in the amount of three; and grind them up, and mix them with an abundance of eggs; if the colour of the patient is hot, use this dressing, for it is of good composition, and restores the broken bones immediately, and is worth more than many homes.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a torsion: pren mugat et vismalve, ana X З.; mirre et aloes, ana VIIII З.; acaice VI З.; bolic armenic X З., et broie bien, et confit avec aigue ou abun d’uef, et l’aministre.	Another method of plastering that is worth for torsion: take mugwort and vinegar, each X parts; myrrh and aloes, each VIIII parts; acacia, VI parts; and rue barren, X parts, and grind them well, and mix with water or eggs, and administer it.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a jointes et as os desevrez de lor leus, et apaise la dolor sorvenant, et le taille usent de l’os, et la briseure: pren lainne novele, et la moille en aisil et en oile cuit, et met sus lou leu.	Another method of reducing dislocations and fractures is to loosen the joints and bones of their own accord, and relieve the pain that arises, and reduce the swelling of the bone, and the bruising. Take new linen, moisten it in oil and water, and put it on the affected area.
Cist emplastres ne restoure mie, mais il est boins por apaisier d’aposteme chaut, et oste proprement les dolors.	These treatments do not restore me, but it is good to appease the after-burning heat, and properly relieve the pains.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre qui restoure les os brisiez: pren foilles de figuier seches, et foilles de pavol terrestre, et broie ensamble, et fai emplaustre.	Other methods of displacement that restore fractured bones: take dry fig leaves and terrestrial pavol leaves, grind them together, and make plaster.</start_of_turn>
Autre maniere d’emplaustre a la restoration de la brisure de l’os et por amenusier lou remenant de l’aposteme.	Another method of reducing dislocations is to restore the break of the bone and mitigate the remaining heat
Pren racine de vismalve, camomille, flors de violettes, farine de veces, de chescune une partie; et broie, et confis avec vin, et se li membres est eschaufez, se le confis avec aigue de coriandre moiste, ou d’aigue, et en use.	Take root of chamomile, camomile, violets flowers, barley flour, and poppyseed; grind them together, and mix with wine, and if the limb is bruised, mix with vinegar or water, and apply it liberally
Autre maniere d’emplaustre plus fors que li premier, quant aposteme dur vient a la restauration dou membre.	Other methods of restoring the limb are more forceful than the first, when an abscess comes to the restoration of the limb.
Pren racines de vismalve, et semence de lin, balauste, mellilote, et sansuce, et flors de violetes et de camomille, de toutes ana une partie; et broie, et confit avec aigue de salz, ou aigue douce, ou avec vin, selonc la chalor du membre, et son repos.	Take root of wormwood, and seed of flax, poultice, melilot, and tansae, and violets and chamomile flowers, of all an equal part; and grind, and confect with salt water, or sweet water, or with wine, according to the heat of the limb and its rest.
Ypocras dist en son livre c’on ne mette sus lou membre brisiet por restorer fuer que cerote fait de cire et d’oile, et nulle autre chose plus, et dist qu’il soit entre dous ne trop molz ne trop durs.	Hippocrates in his book does not put anything on the broken limb to restore force except for some wax and oil, and nothing else, and he says that it should be between soft and firm.</start_of_turn>
Et il semble galien c’on doit mettre sus lou membre brisiet, por restoreir, choses seches un poc chaudes, si com mirre et aloes et encens et semblans choses, si com nos avons dit.	And it seems Galen says we must put something warm on the affected limb to restore it, such as mustard, aloe, incense and the like, as we have said.
Autre maniere d’emplaustre que vaut a lesion et a dolor: pren mugath et chevolz d’omes decopez, ou pennes d’oiseil, et vismalve, et seil igalment, et les broie, et en fai emplaustre et lou lie.	Another way of dressing that is useful for pain and swelling: take mugwort and chopped onions, or poppy seeds, and vinegar, and mix them evenly, and bruise them, and make a poultice from them.</start_of_turn>
Et puis lou deslie un poc aprés, et pren garde se li malaides ne sent nule dolor, ne ne s’est meus li os de son leu; si ne lou deslie par mains jors Et se dolor vient au leu, ou prurite, sens repos ou enfleure, si te haste, et le deslie, et oste l’emplaustre maintenant; et pren drapeil legier, et esponge de mer moiste, et plonge en aigue, et en leve lou leu, tant que li prurite cesse, et soit apaisie li dolor; puis laisse reposer lou membre une houre, puis met sus lainne seche, moillie en aigue ou en oile roset, et lie sus une nut, tant que tu soies seur d’aposteme chaut; et repose li enflammation dou membre, et soit ostee li dolor, puis rencomence la lieure subtil, et un poc d’emplaustre, et ne l’estraindre mie si fort com devant; et subtilie tant qu’il soit sanez.	And then loosen a little afterwards, and take care that the patient feels no pain, that the bones are not displaced from their place; if you do not loosen by hand for days. And if pain comes to the limb, or swelling, sense rest or inflammation, if you are hasty, loosen it, and place the limb in place; and take a light bandage, and a wet sponge of sea salt, and immerse in water, and elevate the limb until the swelling ceases, and the pain is appeased; then leave the limb rest for an hour, and put on dry linen, soaked in water or oil rose, and bind with a nut, until you are sure of the abscess being gone; and rest the inflammation of the limb, and be sure that the pain is gone, then repeat the support and a little displacement, do not restrain so much as before; and be discreet until it is healed
Et se tu vois que aposteme chaus et roujor et dolor et enfleure sont jai osteit dou tout, et tu as mestier d’emplaustre et de liien, si le rencomence, si com tu a fait premier igalment.	If you see that the abscess is swollen, red, painful, and inflamed throughout, and that it persists despite initial treatment, you must repeat the procedure as before.
Et s’il ne vient au membre nient de ceu que nos avons dit, si ne le desliier fuer que aprés III jors ou IIII ou V ou VII; et aucune fois le laisse XX jors, selonc ce c’on voit, tant que li briseure s’aert, et aproche li ajostement de la char sus; adonques lou restraint plus que premier, et li engroisse sa viande, si com j’ai dit devant; et se tu vois lou leu sechier plus que mestier n’est, saiches que norrissemens n’i puet venir; arose lou donques d’aigue chaude tede, toutes les fois que tu lou deslies, et ce soit chescun tier jor, et eslaiche un poc la lieure; par cest ovraige donques cort li norrissemens au membre, et esmende hastanment ceu que font li fol mire, que restourent le membre tortement: c’est malvais, et si est ovraiges paourous, et je n’i voi autre chose que dou lassier et de l’ovrer droitement.	And if nothing has happened to the limb in these three days or four, or five, or seven; do not leave it for twenty days, as we have seen; until the swelling has subsided and the joint approaches its natural position. Then restrain it more than before, and fatten the limb as I have said before; and if you see the limb drying out more than usual, do not hesitate to apply warm water; every time you move it, even slightly, split the ligament; by this means you will shorten the swelling of the limb and restore the limb to its normal position: that is bad, and if there is any doubt, I would rather leave it alone than make things worse.
des diverses manieres de brisure	various methods of fracture
Maintes manieres de brisures sont, et diverses figures, et les causes sont destintees; car il a brisures que sont faites de percussion d’espee, et li colz oste tout l’os jusc’a syfac qui est desoz l’os, si com fait haiche ou fust; et por ceu est apelee ceste maniere de briseure «haichure».	Many are the causes of fractures, and diverse are their figures; for there are fractures which are made by the percussion of a spear, and the collar bone is broken all the way down to the syphus which is depressed by the bone, as in the case of a fracture of the heel or forearm; and for this reason this kind of fracture is called a «haichure».
Ou li espee cope l’os par desus sens percier, et est apelee ceste maniere de brisure «absolue»; et est li plaie de ces .ij. brisures grans ou petite.	For the spear pierces the bone in a reverse direction, and this kind of fracture is called an "absolue"; and the wound of these fractures is great or small
Et s’il i a percussion emboee, ou fendure ou de colz de piere, ou de cheoir sus piere, ou d’autres semblans.	And if there is an embedded percussion, or fracture or snap of a bone, or fracture of the bone above the joint, or other similar things.
Et s’il a brisure persant, qui aproiche jusc’a la toile qui est desoz l’os, ou elle est sus l’os, et est ceste plaie ou grans ou petite.	And if there is a burst wound, which approaches the gut that is below the bone, or it is on the bone, and is this wound great or small.
Et si a plaie qui est reponue en l’os ausi subtil com uns chevous, et est li fendure petite, et por ceu est apelee ceste brisure «capillaire»; et si la plaie qui est faite ou par ferir de piere, ou par cheoir, ou par semblant chose, et fait entrer la piece de l’os aval, et est li leus chevez si com font li chaudelier quant il fierent sus les chaudieres; si entre li piere dedens.	And if a wound which is healed in the bone is as slight as a sheep's fleece, and if the fracture is small, and for this reason this kind of fracture is called a «capillaire»; and if the wound is made by a wound from a stone, or by a thorn, or by anything similar, and if it causes the bone to protrude inwards, and if the bone is bent as when boiling is done on spits over a cauldron; if between the bones inwards.
Et ceu avient plus a sous qui ont ou chief moistes os, si com chief d’enfant; et avient a toutes ces manieres de brisure que pieces menues en sont desevrees si com esteles; et je em parlerai en son leu.	And this often happens under those who have or have eaten moist bones, as in the case of children; and it happens in all these ways that the pieces break off like stars; and I will speak of it in its place.
Et conoissons toutes manieres de briseures par la detraction sus elles, et l’inquisition a tentes, et par la char corrumpue qui est sus.	And we know of all kinds of fractures due to the separation above them, the tension at the edges, and the corrupt marrow that is above.
Li semblance capillaire est conue par ceu que quant on a descovert l’os, et on frote enche sus, si apert li brisure noire.	The blood vessels are recognized by this: when one discovers a bone, and wipes over it, if the black fracture is open
En la cure de la brisure, pren garde as accidens dou malaide, et se tu vois nul malvais accident, si com vomissement de cole, et amission d’entendement, et estension, et paumeson, et fievre ague, et les eulz issir fuer et rougir, et autres semblans em plusors dispositions, si ne puet saner sens doute.	In the treatment of fracture, take care of the patient’s condition, and if you see no other mishap, like vomiting of bile, emission of urine, extension, and paleness, fever and swelling, and other similar things, if not, it is certain that the matter will not recover without doubt.
Et se tu ne vois accident espoantauble, et tu esperes salut, si comence la cure.	And if no sudden accident befalls thee, and thou hopeth for health, if so, commence the treatment.
Et quant li navrez vient premier a toi, et c’est en yver, si covient oster l’os devant lou quatorzime jor au mues que tu pues.	And when the swelling comes first to thee, and it be in the armpit, if so, know that it is necessary to remove the bone from the quadrant above the muscle which thou dost touch.</start_of_turn>
Et se c’est en esteit, si te haste d’oster l’os devant lou septime jor, ainsois que li toile desoz l’os soit corrumpue, et vienent li accident que nos avons dit.	And if it be in this state, if thou hast haste to remove the bone from the seventh quadrant above the bone, thereby that the bone of the armpit be corrupted, and comes the accident which we have said.</start_of_turn
Et se li brisure de l’os vient jusc’a la toile que cuevre lou cerveil, si covient que tu fendes la partie fendue ensi: et est que tu preignes lou chief dou malaide navreit, et descuevre sus l’os en toutes les manieres que tu pues, et sus la figure de la plaie plus souef que tu pues, et se decorrement de sanc vient au descovrir l’os, ou aposteme chaus, se li aide de ceu qu’il covient, et est que tu emplisses lou leu de drapelz moilliez en oile roset; et fai reposer la plaie tant que li apostemes chaus soit apaisiez, et soies asseurez de decorrement de sanc; puis comence entor l’os a oster, et c’est fait selonc II manieres d’ovraige: l’une manieres si est que tu copes au taillement estroit devant, et c’est li forme:	And if the breaking of the bone comes up to the marrow covering the brain, if you must split open the fractured part: and take the head of the sufferer from the navel and uncover the bone in all the ways you can, and on the figure of the wound more roughly than you can; and if blood comes out from uncovering the bone, or incision of the navel, if there is aid in this, and that you fill the cavity with damp cloths soaked in rose oil; and rest the wound until the incisions of the navel are healed, and be sure of the evacuation of blood; then begin to rotate the bone to harden, and this is done in two ways: one way if the incision is narrow at the tailbone, and this is the shape:
Et puis i boute .i. autre taillement plus grant que lou premier.	And then there is another tail that is larger than the first.
Et li seconde maniere si est que tu aies nombre de taillemens divers, les uns estrois et les autres larges, et soient a pointe, et soient de fer inde; et en oevre legierement, que tu ne bleces le chief.	And thus if you have various sizes of wounds, some wide and some narrow, pointed or blunt, and over a wound that is slight, do not close the wound.
Et se li os est fors et durs, si covient que tu lou perces entor, ainsois que tu i aministres taillemens avec tareres, ne mie parfont qu’il ne percent outre l’os dou test, et doit avoir desus la pointe une marge ou .i. cercle, que ne laisse passer la pointe outre l’os; et t’en covient avoir plusors, si que chescuns viegne en son leu de l’espés de l’os, si que tu aies tareres por toz test; et c’est li forme des .iij. manieres de tareres:	And if the bone is strong and hard, if you must pierce through, even if you administer wounds with sponges, do not make them larger than necessary, and must have above the point a margin or circle, which must not pass beyond the bone; and you must have several of these, so that when the flesh comes to the thickness of the bone, if you have sponges throughout, it will fill up the space of the bone. This is the form of the two methods of sponges:
Li qualitez por percier entor l’os brisiet est que tu mettes .i. tarere sus l’os, et torne a tes dois tant que tu saiches que li os est perciez; puis remet lou tarere autre part un poc aprés, et ensi fai tout entor, puis cope as incisoires l’espaice de l’os qui est entre les pertu, et fai ceu plus subtilment que tu pues, et legierement, si com nos avons dit, tant que tu en traies l’os a ta main, ou a aucun des estrumens que tu as si com tenailles, pinceures; et garde que tu ne touchier au cerveil ne au syfac.	The quality for piercing through a brittle bone is that you put on a sponge on the bone, and turn your hands until you feel that the bone is pierced; then put on another sponge in another place a little later, and do all around, and close as the incisors close the space of the bone that is between the holes, and do this more subtly than you can, and lightly, as we have said, until you have brought the bone to your hand, or to any instruments that you have as if you were holding tweezers; and keep your hand away from the brain and the jaw.
Et quant tu avras l’os perciet, et trait, si couvient que tu reses l’apreteit qui est demoree de l’os a tout I autre estrument semblant a un incisoire, mais il covient qu’il soit plus tenves et plus subtilz que li autre incisoire; et s’il demore rien des petis os, ne nulles escailles, si les oste legierement a I des estrumens; puis cure la plaie de tentes et d’oignemens que nos avons dit.	When you have pierced the bone, and treated, if it seems that the socket which is left empty from the bone to all other instruments is similar to a chisel, but that it should be stronger and more subtle than the others; and if nothing of the small bones remains, do not fear, if the large bones are lightly placed against some instruments; then cure the wound with sutures and ointments as we have said.
Et li autre maniere d’ovraige si est cil que Galiiens dist, qui est plus saus; et le loa molt fort, et c’est sa parole.	And another method of work is like the Galicians say, which is more moist; and the load is very strong, and that is its word.
Il covient premier que tu descuevres une partie de l’os, lai ou li brisure apert mues, si que quant tu avras descovert celle partie, si metras desoz lou chief de cest incisoire lenticulaire, de coi ci est li forme:	It is necessary first that you uncover a part of the bone, either the fracture that appears swollen, so that when you have uncovered that part, put on gloves and grasp the handle of this incisor lens, for this is its form.
Et ne trenche mie d’une part, et est li partie ague en II costez, si que li trenchans est devers l’os; puis fier d’un petit maillet sus, si que tu copes legierement l’os tout entor, et te done bien en garde de la toile que est desoz; s’elle est aerse a l’os, si l’oste legierement, car on l’oste bien sens nuisement, et sens paor.	Do not split the bone in two parts, and if the cutting is toward the bone; then hold a small mallet above, so that you can gently press the bone together, and take care of the cloth that is around it; for if it is pressed against the bone, if the bone is lightly held, because it is well placed without damage, and with fear.
Et se li brisure de l’os ne perce jusc’a la toile, et li os est copez tant soulement par desus, et il i a petites pieces, si couvient rere celle asperiteit, et oster les escailles au raseur subtil dont tu dois avoir diverses quantitez, por amenistrer en chescun leu le plus covenauble, selonc l’ovraige et la figure de la brisure.	And if the bone breaks but does not pierce the skin, cut the bone only slightly above; there are small pieces remaining. If you can repair the fracture, remove the sharp fragments with blunt instruments of various sizes, in order to close as many cavities as possible, according to the work and the appearance of the fracture.
Si reis devant de grant raseurs, et aprés de plus subtilz, et ne cesser de ceu faire tant k’il soit bien.	If there are large fragments remaining, and after further bluntness, and it does not stop, continue to do so until it is well done.</start_of_turn>
En chescune des autres tailleures capillaires et brisure subtil, covient que tu amenistres cause selonc son adrecement; et c’est chose que n’est mie reponue a celui qui a mis son estude en cest art.	Concerning other small wounds and fractures, it is necessary to provide the remedy according to its location; and this is something that is not mentioned by him who has studied this art.</start_of_turn>
Et se li syfac est descovers, si covient que tu preignes I drapeil de lin, selonc la quantiteit de la plaie, et le moille en oile roset, et le mete sus la plaie; puis pren I autre drapeil doublé en II ou en III, et le moille en vin et en oile roset, et le met sus le premier drapeil; et fai ceu plus legierement que tu pues, que tu ne grever lou syfac, puis aministre par desus I autre liien, et ne l’estraindre mie fuer que tant k’il teigne la plaie soulement; et le laisse ensi I jor ou II, tant que li apostemes chaus soit apaisiez, puis le deslie, et i aministre medicines si com racines de lis, et farine de veces, et poudre d’encens, et aristologe, et semblans choses; et de ces choses fai poudre, et met sus la plaie, et i aministre adés medicines mondefians sens mordication; et warde que li plaie soit adés nette, que tu n’i laissier mie l’oignement, ne n’i laissier mie assembler bowe, car quant bowe s’asemble sus la toile dou cerveil, si la corront et porrist, et avient au malaide grans malice; car quant on met sus lou syfac chose mordant, si nercist par desus.	If the scab is discovered, then take a strip of linen, according to the size of the wound, and soak it in oil of roses, and place it on the wound; then take another strip of linen doubled in two or three, and soak it in wine and oil of roses, and place it on the first strip; and do this more gently than you think, that you do not damage the scab, then administer by mouth another medicine, and do not restrain it more than is necessary to hold the wound only; and leave it in place for one or two days, until the abscesses have subsided, then remove it, and administer medicines such as radish, alum, and incense, and an apothecary, and similar things; make powder of these things and put it on the wound, and administer again the former medicines without biting. Take care that the wound is not too tight, that you do not leave it too loose, for if it is too loose it will gather on the brain, and cause great harm; for when anything biting is placed on the scab, if it is not done cautiously.
Pren warde donques, se nulz mavais accidens vient au malaide, si com nos avons dit, si est doutable sens doute.	Beware then, if no other misfortune befalls the patient, as we have said, if there is any doubt or suspicion.
Et se noiror n’i vient fuers que por la medicine c’on a mise sus, que teil vertu ait, si covient que tu preignes une partie de miel, et III parties d’oile roset et en glue I drapel, et lou met sus lou syfac; puis lou cure de manieres de cures qu’il couvient, tant qu’il soit sanez.	And if black bile does not come forth except by the medicine we have set, that some virtue has, if you take a part of honey, and three parts of rose oil and in glue one drapel, and you put it on the affected area; then you cure it in the manner of cures that it is possible, until it is healed.
Saiches que aucune fois sont brisies les .ij. parties dou neis desus, ou li une par li soulement, car ce sont .ij. os; et cil desoz est cartillaginous, et ne brise mie, si ne fait fuer k’enbower ou tordre, et puis saine.	Then comes the treatment in the manner of treatment that is suitable, as long as it is sane.
Et se l’une des II parties est brisie, si covient que tu boutes ton petit doi ou pertus dou neis, et met igal cele brisure dedens a ton doi indice, et au pouchet par defuer, tant que li neis reveigne selonc sa figure natureil; et fai ce legierement, et warde que li malaides ne sente dolor par ton ovraige.	And if one of the two parts is broken, if you can swallow your small dose or wound that does not please you, and put this break even with your index finger and at the wrist, as long as it does not return to its natural figure; and do this gently, and keep that the patient does not feel pain from your work.
Et se li brisure est en la partie desus lou neis, et ne puet venir li dois au leu, si couvient que tu lou mettes igal au chief d’un rait un poc gros.	And if the fracture is on the upper part of the limb, and it cannot be reached from below, it is proper that you place it even with the head of a splint as wide as a thumb.
Et se li brisure est de II pars, si refai autretel, et te haste de restorer lou premier jor, se tu pues; se ce non, aprés lou septime ou le disime, quant li apostemes chaus est apaisiez, puis bouthe au chief dou neis tentes de drapelz de lin, se li brisure est en une partie.	And if the break is of two parts, if you do it again, and hasten to restore the first part if you wish; if not, after the seventh or the tenth day, when the abscess has subsided, then bathe the head that does not please you with cloths of linen, if the break is in a limb.
Ou tu boutes .i. autre d’autre part, se li brisure i est; et soient les tentes si grosses qu’eles emplissent les pertus dou neis.	Or if you must bind it elsewhere, if the fracture is there; and let the attempts be so great that they fill the hole in the limb.
Et dirent aucun restorour c’on doit moillier les tentes em burre, et muer chescun jor.	And they say no restorer shall move the bandages from the wound, and must die each one for himself.
Il ne me semble mie que ce soit bien, mais covient qu’elles soient moillies en confection d’aubun d’uef, et de poudre de molin; puis laisse la tente, tant que li os soit fers, et li cartilaiges endurcis.	I do not seem to agree with this, but it seems proper that they should be bound with egg white and wheat flour; then leave the bandage until the bone is firm and the cartilage is hardened.
Et aucune fois boute on ou neis pennes d’oie en leu de tentes, et si a .i. souef drapeil entor, et se tient la briseure, et soffle li malaides parmi; or fai lou kel ke tu vues.	And never again shall one put goose feathers instead of tents, and if indeed there is snow hanging overhead, and the wind blows, and blows the patient among; or do as you please.</start_of_turn>
Et se apostemes chaus vient au neis tandis com tu i oevres, si i met cerote ou coton moilliet en oile roset, ou en .i. poc de dyaquilon.	And if someone’s clothes come to the neck while you are working, if you put some butter or cotton soaked in rose oil, or some potage of alum.
Et se apostemes chaus n’i vient, si met emplastre par defuer, de farine de sisament, et de poudre d’encens, confites avec aubun d’uef, puis met sus estoupes legieres, et ne liier mie lou neis; et se li brisure dou neis est petite, et il est brisiés em menues pieces, si l’estrai as estrumens covenables a ceu, puis cout la fendure, et la cure de choses enjanrans char, et soudant, si com emplastres et oignemens covenables a ceu; et s’il n’a dedens lou neis nule plaie, si lou cure de tentes, et i met cainnes de plonc, tant qu’il soit sanez.
